[tool: Read] {"file_path":"/Users/agoodkind/Sites/configs.wt-ifmgr-scaffold/docs/opnsense/daemon.md"}
[tool output]
1	# OPNsense OOB daemon
2
3	`mwan-opnsense` is the out-of-band (OOB) control channel into the production OPNsense
4	router. It exists so you can reach OPNsense when its network stack is down. Treat it as a
5	break-glass lever that must work 100% of the time, because the one moment you need it is
6	the moment nothing else works.
7
8	This document explains why the daemon is built the way it is. The code is the source of
9	truth for what it does; this doc records the durable reasons so a change is never made
10	blind. For the live production role and topology, see [production OPNsense state](../infra/opnsense.md)
11	and [emergency out-of-band access](../infra/oob.md).
12
13	## Why it exists
14
15	The daemon gives you gRPC-style access to OPNsense over a serial channel, with no
16	dependency on the network stack.
17
18	- No SSH. SSH breaks across upgrades and depends on the very netstack that may be down.
19	- No package manager. The daemon ships as a single self-contained ELF binary, dropped onto
20	  the guest from the Proxmox host.
21	- Rate its blast radius by the failure it exists for, not by normal operation. In an
22	  outage every other path is gone and this serial daemon is the only way into OPNsense.
23
24	A multi-year roadmap rides on this channel, so its reliability during a failure is the
25	whole design goal.
26
27	## Topology
28
29	Three processes connect the operator to the OPNsense config.
30
31	1. A probe (the `mwan` monolith) dials a local unix socket on the Proxmox host.
32	2. The host bridge (`mwan-opnsense-host`, a systemd service with `Restart=always`) forwards
33	   that connection over a yamux session to the guest, across the qemu virtio-serial chardev.
34	3. The daemon (`mwan-opnsense` on FreeBSD) serves gRPC multiplexed over that yamux session,
35	   reading and writing `/conf/config.xml` and running guest commands.
36
37	The host-side bridge lives in `cmd/mwan/opnsense_host.go`. The guest-side serve loop lives
38	in `internal/opnsensesvc/serve.go`. The wire contract is `proto/mwan/v1/mwan_opnsense.proto`.
39
40	## The serial transport and its invariants
41
42	The transport is qemu virtio-serial, because FreeBSD has no vsock host support yet. The
43	device is a FreeBSD tty, which fights binary traffic in several ways, so the code holds a
44	set of hard-won invariants. Each one prevents a wedge that the May 2026 bring-up fought for
45	days. Do not change them without reading this section.
46
47	- The persistent file descriptor (fd) is never closed during daemon life. `serialStream.Close`
48	  in `serial_listener.go` is a deliberate no-op so the fd outlives every yamux session; the
49	  serve loop rebuilds the yamux and gRPC layers over the same chardev when a session ends.
50	  The fd is closed only when the daemon exits.
51	- The device opens in raw mode with software flow control off. Arbitrary ELF bytes contain
52	  the XON and XOFF control codes, so leaving `IXON` or `IXOFF` set lets the tty eat binary
53	  payload and hang a transfer. See `serial_open_freebsd.go`.
54	- Each write is capped at 8 KiB. The FreeBSD tty input queue is sized from the baud rate; a
55	  single write larger than the queue silently loses its tail. The cap and the chunking loop
56	  live in `serial_listener.go`.
57	- The transfer protocol paces with stop-and-wait acknowledgements, one chunk outstanding at
58	  a time, so the qemu virtio receive queue cannot overflow. See `internal/opnsensesvc/transfer.go`.
59	- The daemon runs `TIOCFLUSH` on open to drop bytes left in the tty queue by a prior
60	  session. The driver does not flush on close, so without this the leftover frames corrupt
61	  the next session's handshake. See `serial_open_freebsd.go`.
62	- The daemon binds the named virtio port `io.goodkind.mwan-opnsense.0`, never the raw device
63	  `/dev/ttyV0.1`. The QEMU Guest Agent (QGA) shares the raw device numbering, so binding the
64	  raw path lets the two collide and steal each other's port. The named port also survives
65	  slot renumbering across reboots.
66
67	The deeper driver rationale (no host-disconnect signal, the tty queue draining only at fd
68	refcount zero) comes from the MWAN-95 design investigation. The repo code embodies the
69	resulting behavior; the strongest local proof is the stop test, where the serial read
70	provably never returns on its own.
71
72	## Transport history
73
74	The transport has been reversed twice and is best understood as hard-won, not arbitrary.
75
76	1. A TCP-listener-style model opened and closed the device per session. It broke on the
77	   second session because of the tty close-flush and a qemu open-event race.
78	2. A custom MWN1 framing layer (length-prefixed, correlation-id multiplexed) was proven in a
79	   proof of concept, then reverted.
80	3. The live transport is yamux over one persistent fd, with gRPC multiplexed on top. It
81	   landed in commit `91fcbe2` on 2026-05-12 and has been stable since.
82
83	The long-term direction is AF_VSOCK once FreeBSD supports it, which would delete this whole
84	serial apparatus.
85
86	Note for anyone reading the May transcripts: a real self-`exec` re-exec existed in the MWN1
87	era and was removed in the yamux pivot. The current daemon does not re-exec, and the proto
88	has no re-exec field. Do not reintroduce it from old material.
89
90	## Lifecycle and the self-deploy round-trip
91
92	The daemon starts from rc.d, serves until its context is cancelled, then exits. The success
93	criterion for any stop or self-deploy is the round-trip: the lever must come back up and
94	serve the intended binary, and a bad binary must auto-revert. "Exits cleanly and stays down"
95	is a failure for a break-glass lever.
96
97	- Startup. The rc.d script templates the daemon config, runs the preflight check, then
98	  starts the daemon under FreeBSD `daemon(8)`. The serve loop opens the serial device once.
99	- Self-deploy. The operator pushes a new binary over the transfer service, stages it (an
100	  atomic swap of the `.current` and `.previous` slots plus a pending-verify marker), then
101	  asks the daemon to restart. The daemon exits and is respawned onto the new binary. The
102	  rc.d preflight reverts to `.previous` unless the daemon stamped `health=ok`, so a bad
103	  deploy heals itself. Config writes are atomic, so a hard exit never leaves a half-written
104	  config. See `internal/opnsensesvc/deploy.go` and `server.go`.
105	- Restart. `RestartDaemon` cancels the serve context; the daemon exits and the supervisor
106	  respawns it. There is no in-process re-exec.
107
108	## The stop-orphan defect and its fix
109
110	The daemon could hang on stop and orphan its child process, which is the defect this work
111	fixes. The chain: on stop the serve context is cancelled, the shutdown path calls the yamux
112	`Session.Close`, and `Session.Close` blocks waiting for the read loop to exit. The read loop
113	is parked in a blocking serial read that never returns, because the driver gives no
114	disconnect signal. The process hangs, rc.d times out and sends `SIGKILL` to the `daemon(8)`
115	supervisor, and the wedged child survives holding the serial device.
116
117	The fix has two coupled halves.
118
119	1. Clean bounded exit. On terminal exit the daemon closes the real serial fd to unblock the
120	   read loop, bounds the graceful stop, and forces the process to exit as a last resort. The
121	   normal path still returns cleanly so no cleanup is skipped.
122	2. Respawn with preflight. The rc.d script supervises with `daemon -r` over a small shim that
123	   runs the preflight then execs the daemon, so a restart round-trips and a bad self-deploy
124	   auto-reverts. The forced stop kills the process group so a wedged child can never orphan.
125
126	## Session recovery after a host-side disruption
127
128	When the host bridge or the chardev drainer restarts or dies mid-transfer, the guest keeps
129	its serial fd open and its old yamux session live, because the byte stream never breaks. The
130	host re-dials and opens a fresh yamux session over the same stream. The guest's old session
131	reads the new session's first framing as a collision and ends with the error
132	`grpc serve: duplicate stream initiated`, and the serve loop rebuilds. Recovery is driven by
133	that collision event, not by a timer, and is observed to complete in under five seconds
134	across testbed accumulation runs (162 genuine kill-mid-transfer trials, zero wedges, zero
135	stalls).
136
137	This is why no liveness frames are added to the serial stream and yamux keepalive stays off
138	(see [What not to touch](#what-not-to-touch)). Recovery does not depend on the host bridge
139	heartbeat firing; the heartbeat is a backstop, and the common path is the immediate
140	duplicate-stream rebuild.
141
142	To inspect the guest's serial reads and writes at the syscall level, run dtrace on the
143	OPNsense guest with `-xnolibs`. The bundled dtrace libraries (`ip.d`, `socket.d`) fail to
144	compile on this kernel for lack of CTF, so the default invocation aborts; `-xnolibs` skips
145	the libraries and the `syscall` provider still works.
146
147	## What not to touch
148
149	These are the load-bearing invariants. Changing any of them re-opens a documented wedge.
150
151	- The no-op `serialStream.Close` and the never-close-during-life rule.
152	- The termios setup (`VMIN=1`, `VTIME=0`, `IXON` and `IXOFF` cleared).
153	- The 8 KiB write cap and the transfer ack pacing.
154	- The yamux tuning and the keepalive-off setting.
155	- The named virtio port; never bind the raw `/dev/ttyV0.1`.
156	- The `TIOCFLUSH`-on-open and the session-rebuild loop.
157
158	## Operating it
159
160	Two guides cover the daemon end to end:
161
162	- [install](install.md) brings up OPNsense and the daemon on a fresh VM.
163	- [operations](operations.md) covers updating the binary, the firmware-upgrade use case,
164	  and recovering a wedged channel.
165	- [wedge](wedge.md) explains the mid-transfer write wedge: its trigger, the evidenced
166	  mechanism, recovery, and how to inspect a wedged guest with a kernel-debug build.
167
168	## Further reading
169
170	The build-up and the prior wedges are tracked under the MWAN project: the v0 epic (MWAN-90),
171	the serial listener (MWAN-95), the wedge tickets (MWAN-184, MWAN-116, MWAN-113, MWAN-114),
172	the post-rollback redial (MWAN-178), and the round-trip fix this doc accompanies (MWAN-3).

### Assistant (2026-07-06 21:07)
[tool: Read] {"file_path":"/Users/agoodkind/Sites/configs.wt-ifmgr-scaffold/docs/opnsense/operations.md"}
[tool output]
1	# Operate the OPNsense OOB daemon
2
3	This guide covers the day-to-day operations you run against the `mwan-opnsense`
4	out-of-band (OOB) daemon: update its binary, upgrade the OPNsense firmware through it,
5	and recover it when a channel wedges. For why the daemon is built the way it is, read
6	[OPNsense OOB daemon](daemon.md). To install it on a fresh VM, read [install](install.md).
7
8	Exact command flags drift, so this guide describes behavior and names the verbs. Run
9	`mwan opnsense <verb> --help` for the current flag set; that help text is the source of
10	truth.
11
12	## Pick the right channel
13
14	The daemon is one of three OOB channels into OPNsense, and each fits a different job.
15
16	- The gRPC-over-serial daemon is reliable for short calls: `version`, `state`, xpath
17	  reads, and the upgrade validation matrix. Prefer it for control-plane operations,
18	  because it survives a packet-filter or routing break that would silently fail the
19	  guest agent.
20	- The QEMU Guest Agent (QGA) is fine for read-only probes such as `qm guest exec`.
21	- SSH over the privileged admin path carries large file pushes and long exec runs better
22	  than the serial daemon does.
23	- The serial console is the kernel-level last resort. It is the only signal that survives
24	  a kernel panic, a botched bootloader, or lost network state.
25
26	## Update the daemon binary
27
28	You update the daemon over the same serial channel it serves, so the update works even
29	when the network is down. The flow is push, stage, restart, verify.
30
31	1. Push the new binary to the staging slot: `mwan opnsense daemon push <binary>`.
32	2. Promote it: `mwan opnsense daemon stage <sha256>`. This swaps the new binary into the
33	   active slot, keeps the previous binary as the rollback slot, and drops a
34	   pending-verify marker.
35	3. Restart onto it: `mwan opnsense daemon restart`. The daemon exits and the supervisor
36	   respawns it on the new binary.
37	4. Verify: `mwan opnsense daemon version` shows the new commit, and `mwan opnsense daemon
38	   state` shows the active and previous hashes and the health.
39
40	A new binary that never reports healthy auto-reverts to the previous binary on the next
41	respawn, so a bad self-deploy heals itself. To revert by hand, run `mwan opnsense daemon
42	revert`. The daemon stamps itself healthy once it serves cleanly, which clears the
43	pending-verify marker so later restarts keep the new binary.
44
45	## Use case: upgrade the OPNsense firmware
46
47	The `mwan opnsense upgrade` verb drives a firmware upgrade as a state machine, so each
48	phase records an artifact and refuses to run out of order. Run the phases by hand the
49	first time on a target, then use one-shot mode for repeat cycles.
50
51	The phases run in order.
52
53	1. `prepare`: take a Proxmox snapshot, capture the pre-upgrade config and routing state,
54	   and move the state to prepared.
55	2. `execute`: run the in-guest upgrade, stream its log, and reboot onto the new firmware.
56	3. `validate`: run the post-upgrade check matrix and compare it against the pre-upgrade
57	   baseline.
58	4. `commit` or `rollback`: commit deletes the snapshot and locks the cycle; rollback
59	   restores the snapshot and re-validates.
60
61	Use `mwan opnsense upgrade run` to chain prepare, execute, and validate under one set of
62	flags, with auto-rollback on a hard failure. Use it only after a manual cycle has
63	succeeded on that target, so you have seen each artifact land.
64
65	Prefer the gRPC transport for the upgrade so the control path survives a routing break
66	during the upgrade itself. Proxmox-host and LAN-client checks still run over SSH, because
67	the daemon does not proxy those surfaces.
68
69	Watch three signals during the execute window, which runs 10 to 30 minutes:
70
71	- The upgrade log the daemon streams to its state directory.
72	- The OPNsense system log over the admin SSH path, which drops during the reboot and
73	  returns when the guest is back.
74	- The serial console, which is the only signal that survives a panic or a failed reboot.
75
76	## Take snapshots without saved RAM
77
78	Always take testbed snapshots with `--vmstate 0`. A snapshot that includes RAM resumes on
79	rollback with a stale wall clock, dead TCP sockets, and a stale resolver cache, which
80	produced hours of confusing failures in past sessions. Production OPNsense never uses RAM
81	snapshots. The web GUI defaults RAM snapshots on for a running VM, so do not take
82	snapshots from the GUI for this work.
83
84	## Recover a wedged daemon
85
86	The daemon can wedge under a heavy exec or a large stdin payload. Recover in order of
87	least disruption.
88
89	1. Retry over SSH or the serial console for the operation that wedged it, since those
90	   channels do not share the daemon's state.
91	2. Reset the guest with `qm reset <vmid>`. This recovers the guest agent and usually
92	   recovers the daemon.
93	3. If the daemon stays down, reach the Proxmox host over the pinned OOB tunnel at
94	   `root@3d06:bad:b01:ff::1` and restart the service from there.
95	4. For a guest that will not come back over any network path, attach the serial console
96	   and recover at the kernel level.
97
98	After a `qm rollback`, the guest reboots and the daemon starts with it. The daemon is
99	reachable as soon as the virtio-serial socket is up, which is the same liveness signal the
100	upgrade validator waits on.
101

1	# Install OPNsense with the OOB daemon
2
3	This guide creates a fresh OPNsense VM and brings it to a working state with SSH, the
4	QEMU Guest Agent (QGA), and the `mwan-opnsense` out-of-band (OOB) daemon. For why the
5	daemon is built the way it is, read [OPNsense OOB daemon](daemon.md). To operate the
6	daemon once it runs, read [operations](operations.md).
7
8	Run every command from the operator workstation against the target Proxmox host. The
9	examples use placeholders: set `VMID`, `LAN_IP`, and `PVE` (the Proxmox host) once and
10	reuse them. Test it on the suburban testbed, never against production or an existing
11	router VM.
12
13	## Stay inside the safety boundary
14
15	A wrong VMID destroys the wrong machine, so confirm the target before any destructive
16	command.
17
18	- Use an unused VMID and a unique LAN address on the testbed subnet.
19	- Before each create, set, or delete, confirm the command names only your chosen VMID.
20	- If an installer prompt is ambiguous, stop instead of guessing.
21
22	Confirm the VMID is free first:
23
24	```bash
25	ssh "$PVE" "qm list; qm config $VMID 2>&1 || true"
26	```
27
28	Proceed only if the VMID has no existing config.
29
30	## Create the VM with a named serial port
31
32	Create the VM with a serial console, QGA enabled, one management interface, and a separate
33	named virtio-serial port for the daemon. The named port is load-bearing: it keeps the
34	daemon off the raw device that the guest agent uses, so the two never collide.
35
36	```bash
37	ssh "$PVE" "qm create $VMID \
38	    --name opnsense \
39	    --memory 4096 --cores 2 --ostype other \
40	    --scsihw virtio-scsi-pci \
41	    --serial0 socket --vga serial0 \
42	    --net0 virtio,bridge=vmbr1 \
43	    --agent enabled=1 \
44	    --args '-device virtio-serial-pci,id=mwanrpc -chardev socket,id=mwanchr,path=/var/run/qemu-server/$VMID.mwanrpc,server=on,wait=off -device virtserialport,chardev=mwanchr,name=io.goodkind.mwan-opnsense.0'"
45	```
46
47	Attach the serial installer image, create a 16G or larger target disk, and boot the
48	installer. An 8G disk fails the ZFS install with `gpart: autofill: No space left on
49	device`.
50
51	```bash
52	ssh "$PVE" "qm set $VMID --scsi0 local-zfs:0,import-from=/var/lib/vz/template/iso/OPNsense-serial-amd64.img"
53	ssh "$PVE" "qm set $VMID --scsi1 local-zfs:16,discard=on"
54	ssh "$PVE" "qm set $VMID --boot order=scsi0"
55	ssh "$PVE" "qm start $VMID"
56	```
57
58	## Install OPNsense over the serial console
59
60	Attach a TTY to the serial console. A non-TTY `qm terminal` fails with `tcgetattr`.
61
62	```bash
63	ssh -tt "$PVE" "qm terminal $VMID"
64	```
65
66	Wait for the `login:` prompt, then log in as `installer` with password `opnsense`. Walk
67	the installer with these choices:
68
69	1. Keymap: continue with the default.
70	2. Install: choose Install (ZFS), stripe.
71	3. Disk: select the QEMU target with Space, then press Enter directly. Do not press Tab
72	   first, because Tab moves focus to Back.
73	4. Finish: choose Complete Install, then Halt.
74
75	After the VM halts, boot the installed disk:
76
77	```bash
78	ssh "$PVE" "qm set $VMID --boot order=scsi1"
79	ssh "$PVE" "qm start $VMID"
80	```
81
82	## Configure the LAN and enable SSH
83
84	Use console menu option 2 to set the LAN address. Decline DHCP, set the address and
85	subnet, leave the gateway empty, and decline the DHCP server. The console then shows the
86	web GUI URL and the LAN interface address.
87
88	Enable SSH from the shell (menu option 8). Start `/bin/sh` first, because the root shell
89	is `csh`:
90
91	```sh
92	sh
93	sysrc openssh_enable=YES
94	sysrc openssh_skipportscheck=YES
95	service openssh start
96	```
97
98	Confirm SSH from the Proxmox host before continuing:
99
100	```bash
101	ssh "$PVE" "nc -vz -w 3 $LAN_IP 22"
102	```
103
104	## Enable the guest agent
105
106	Install the guest agent, and upgrade the package set first. The OPNsense image ships a
107	package set frozen at release time, and the mirror ships a current set. Installing against
108	the frozen set produces an application binary interface (ABI) skew that breaks tools such
109	as `vtysh`. Running `pkg upgrade -y` first keeps the set self-consistent.
110
111	```sh
112	route add default <gateway> || true
113	printf 'nameserver 1.1.1.1\n' >/etc/resolv.conf
114	pkg update -f
115	pkg upgrade -y
116	pkg install -y os-qemu-guest-agent
117	sysrc qemu_guest_agent_enable=YES
118	service qemu-guest-agent start
119	```
120
121	Confirm the agent answers from the Proxmox host:
122
123	```bash
124	ssh "$PVE" "qm guest exec $VMID -- /bin/hostname"
125	```
126
127	## Generate an API key
128
129	OPNsense API access needs a key and secret tied to a user. Generate them with the
130	`createKey` method, and never echo either value to logs or chat.
131
132	```sh
133	cat > /tmp/create_api_key.php <<'PHP'
134	<?php
135	chdir('/usr/local/opnsense/mvc');
136	require_once('script/load_phalcon.php');
137	use OPNsense\Auth\API;
138	$api = new API();
139	$result = $api->createKey('root');
140	if ($result === false) { fwrite(STDERR, "createKey returned false\n"); exit(1); }
141	echo $result['key'] . "\0" . $result['secret'];
142	PHP
143	php /tmp/create_api_key.php
144	```
145
146	The output is the key and secret separated by a null byte. Split it into two mode-600
147	files on the host. Confirm the pair against the firmware status endpoint; a 200 response
148	means the key is active.
149
150	```bash
151	curl -k -u "$KEY:$SECRET" "https://$LAN_IP/api/core/firmware/status"
152	```
153
154	## Install the daemon
155
156	Build the artifacts from `mwan/go` with the Makefile:
157
158	```bash
159	make build-mwan-opnsense build-linux
160	```
161
162	Copy the daemon binary and its service files onto the guest:
163
164	- `bin/mwan-opnsense` to `/usr/local/sbin/mwan-opnsense.current`
165	- `cmd/mwan/opnsense-src/etc/rc.d/mwan_opnsense` to `/usr/local/etc/rc.d/mwan_opnsense`
166	- `cmd/mwan/opnsense-src/etc/rc.conf.d/mwan_opnsense.sample` to `/etc/rc.conf.d/mwan_opnsense`
167	- `cmd/mwan/opnsense-src/boot/loader.conf.d/mwan_opnsense.conf` to `/boot/loader.conf.d/mwan_opnsense.conf`
168
169	Point the daemon at the named port, symlink the active slot, and start it:
170
171	```sh
172	chmod 0555 /usr/local/sbin/mwan-opnsense.current /usr/local/etc/rc.d/mwan_opnsense
173	ln -sf /usr/local/sbin/mwan-opnsense.current /usr/local/sbin/mwan-opnsense
174	sysrc mwan_opnsense_enable=YES
175	service mwan_opnsense start
176	service mwan_opnsense status
177	```
178
179	Confirm the named port maps to the device, with the guest agent on its own port:
180
181	```text
182	/dev/vtcon/io.goodkind.mwan-opnsense.0 -> ../ttyV0.1
183	/dev/vtcon/org.qemu.guest_agent.0 -> ../ttyV1.1
184	```
185
186	## Verify the channel
187
188	Run the host bridge on the Proxmox host and probe the daemon. The bridge reads its
189	upstream and listen sockets from `[opnsense.host]` in `/etc/mwan/config.toml`, and the
190	probe reads its target from `[opnsense.probe]`.
191
192	```bash
193	mwan opnsense host serve
194	mwan opnsense daemon version
195	mwan opnsense exec /bin/hostname
196	```
197
198	The version call returns the daemon's build banner, and the exec returns the guest
199	hostname.
200
201	## Validate after a reboot
202
203	Reboot the VM and re-run the checks. SSH and the guest agent can be briefly unavailable
204	right after a reboot even when the serial console already shows the login prompt, so wait
205	before you judge a failure.
206
207	```bash
208	ssh "$PVE" "qm reboot $VMID"
209	ssh "$PVE" "qm guest exec $VMID -- /bin/hostname"
210	mwan opnsense daemon version
211	```
212
213	The install is done when the guest agent answers, the daemon reports its version, and the
214	named port still maps to the device.

[tool output]
1	# The mid-transfer write wedge
2
3	A bridge disconnect can hang the guest when the guest is writing to the host. The guest stays
4	hung until you hard reset the VM. This page covers the trigger, the mechanism, recovery, and how
5	to reproduce and inspect the wedge. For the daemon and transport it constrains, see
6	[the OOB daemon doc](daemon.md).
7
8	The mechanism below is the best-supported explanation of how the wedge happens.
9
10	## Trigger
11
12	The wedge needs a guest-to-host write in flight when the host side drops.
13
14	A `file pull` or a large command output keeps the guest writing toward the host. A bridge restart
15	or crash during one can wedge the guest. A `file push` rarely wedges, because the guest is mostly
16	reading.
17
18	## Symptoms
19
20	The gRPC channel stops answering. Both guest vCPUs peg in the kernel. sshd, the QEMU Guest Agent,
21	and the serial console stop responding, because no thread gets a CPU slice.
22
23	## Mechanism
24
25	A single guest write strands in the kernel.
26
27	1. A guest write to the virtio-serial port enters the FreeBSD driver. The driver takes the port
28	   mutex `vtcpmtx` and busy-spins in `virtqueue_poll`, waiting for the host to consume the
29	   buffer. `info registers` over the qemu monitor shows a vCPU stuck in `virtqueue_poll`.
30	2. The host chardev disconnects mid-write. qemu does not complete the in-flight descriptor, so
31	   the poll never returns. `info chardev` shows the port `disconnected` while the write never
32	   finishes. This is a known qemu limitation through qemu 11.0 (Red Hat bug 1352977).
33	3. A second guest thread needs `vtcpmtx` and busy-spins for it. The other vCPU sits in
34	   `lock_delay`. A guest memory dump shows `vtcpmtx` held by the stuck write thread.
35
36	Both vCPUs then spin in the kernel and the guest starves. The spinning write is uninterruptible,
37	so you cannot signal or kill it.
38
39	## Recovery
40
41	Run `qm reset <vmid>`. The hard reset recreates the qemu device and clears the wedge. A bridge
42	restart alone does not clear it, and neither does a guest daemon restart.
43
44	To avoid the wedge, do not restart the bridge while a deploy or any guest-to-host transfer is in
45	flight.
46
47	## Fix
48
49	The fix is a persistent host-side drainer. The drainer holds the chardev open and always reads
50	it, so the host side stays connected and guest writes complete while the bridge restarts behind
51	it. The bridge dials the drainer instead of the chardev.
52
53	The production version is not yet implemented. Implement it only from a written design committed
54	under `docs/superpowers/specs/`, never ad hoc.
55
56	## Reproduce
57
58	Reproduce the wedge on a testbed VM, never on production.
59
60	1. Start a guest-to-host transfer that keeps the guest writing, such as a `file pull` of a large
61	   file.
62	2. A few seconds later, while the transfer is mid-flight, restart or kill the host bridge. The
63	   chardev then disconnects under the guest's write.
64	3. Read the guest from the hypervisor with `info registers` over the qemu monitor. A wedge shows
65	   both vCPUs in the kernel (`CPL=0`, `HLT=0`), one in `virtqueue_poll` and one in `lock_delay`.
66	   A healthy guest shows both vCPUs halted at the idle loop.
67
68	A pull wedges far more readily than a push. Recover with `qm reset <vmid>` between attempts. The
69	reset reboots the guest and clears the tmpfs scratch file.
70
71	## Inspect a wedged guest
72
73	Userland is starved, so sshd, the guest agent, and the serial-console shell do not respond. Use
74	the kernel debugger instead.
75
76	Set `debug.kdb.break_to_debugger=1` on the guest. Run `echo nmi | qm monitor <vmid>` from the
77	Proxmox host. The guest enters DDB on the serial console and dumps every thread's backtrace. Read
78	the output over the `serial0` socket.
79
80	DDB on the stock kernel shows each thread's stack, but it cannot name the owner of a contended
81	lock. The stock OPNsense kernel ships without `WITNESS`. To attribute `vtcpmtx` to the thread
82	that holds it, build a debug kernel.
83
84	## Build a WITNESS debug kernel
85
86	A WITNESS kernel lets DDB print lock ownership with `show alllocks` and `show lockchain`. That
87	turns "a thread is spinning in `lock_delay`" into "this thread holds `vtcpmtx`". The stock kernel
88	already has `DDB`, `KDB`, and `DDB_CTF`. You add only `WITNESS` and `DEBUG_LOCKS`.
89
90	Match the source to the running kernel. OPNsense builds its kernel from `github.com/opnsense/src`
91	at the tag for the release. For OPNsense 26.1.2 (FreeBSD 14.3-RELEASE-p8) the tag is `26.1.1`.
92	Confirm the version with `sysctl kern.conftxt` and the kernel git hash it prints.
93
94	1. Free disk. The build needs 5 to 10 GB. Grow the VM disk from the Proxmox host if needed with
95	   `qm disk resize <vmid> scsi0 +24G`. Then on the guest, run `gpart recover da0`,
96	   `gpart resize -i 4 da0`, and `zpool online -e zroot da0p4`.
97	2. Stage the source. The guest has no internet. Clone `opnsense/src` at the tag on the host and
98	   copy it into the guest `/usr/src`, or clone directly if the guest can reach GitHub.
99	3. Write the config. In `/usr/src/sys/amd64/conf`, create a file named `SMP-WITNESS`:
100
101	   ```
102	   include SMP
103	   ident SMP-WITNESS
104	   options WITNESS
105	   options DEBUG_LOCKS
106	   makeoptions DEBUG=-g
107	   ```
108
109	4. Build the kernel with `make -j2 buildkernel KERNCONF=SMP-WITNESS`. On a 2-vCPU VM this takes
110	   about an hour.
111	5. Install beside the stock kernel, never over it:
112	   `make installkernel KERNCONF=SMP-WITNESS KODIR=/boot/kernel.witness`. The stock `/boot/kernel`
113	   stays as the fallback.
114	6. Boot it deliberately. The `nextboot` one-shot selector may not pick the debug kernel on this
115	   loader. Set `kernel="kernel.witness"` in `/boot/loader.conf.local` instead, or pick the kernel
116	   at the loader prompt over the `serial0` console.
117	7. Verify the debug kernel is live. `uname -i` prints `SMP-WITNESS`, and `sysctl debug.witness.watch`
118	   exists. That OID is absent on the stock kernel.
119	8. Capture lock ownership. Induce a wedge, enter DDB with `echo nmi | qm monitor <vmid>`, then run
120	   `show alllocks` and `show lockchain` to read which thread owns `vtcpmtx`.
121	9. Revert. Remove the `kernel="kernel.witness"` line from `/boot/loader.conf.local` and reboot to
122	   the stock kernel. The stock `/boot/kernel` was never modified.
123

[tool: Read] {"file_path":"/Users/agoodkind/Sites/configs.wt-ifmgr-scaffold/docs/infra/wireguard-roaming.md"}
[tool output]
1	# WireGuard endpoint roaming research notes
2
3	Sources read: `wireguard-go` (Jason Donenfeld's userspace reference impl,
4	~~/Sites/wireguard-go), `wireguard-tools` man pages (~~/Sites/wireguard-tools/src/man),
5	WG protocol description ([https://www.wireguard.com/protocol/](https://www.wireguard.com/protocol/)).
6
7	## What the protocol does
8
9	From `wg(8)` man page (canonical):
10
11	> Endpoint, an endpoint IP or hostname, followed by a colon, and then a port
12	> number. **This endpoint will be updated automatically to the most recent source
13	> IP address and port of correctly authenticated packets from the peer.** Optional.
14
15	This is enforced at the protocol layer. Every authenticated packet causes the
16	receiver to overwrite the peer's stored endpoint with the source IP and port of
17	the received packet. This includes handshake init, handshake response, transport
18	packets, and keepalives.
19
20	Source path in `wireguard-go`:
21
22	- `device/receive.go:374`. Handshake init: `peer.SetEndpointFromPacket(elem.endpoint)`.
23	- `device/receive.go:401`. Handshake response: same.
24	- `device/receive.go:459`. First authenticated transport: same.
25	- `device/receive.go:515`. Every subsequent authenticated transport: same.
26	- `device/peer.go:279-287`. `SetEndpointFromPacket` locks endpoint and overwrites
27	`peer.endpoint.val` with the new endpoint.
28
29	There is a `disableRoaming` flag at `device/peer.go:32`. It cannot be set via
30	standard config. No standard `wg`/`wg-quick` config keyword sets it. It is only
31	set true via private API `device.DisableSomeRoamingForBrokenMobileSemantics()`
32	at `device/mobilequirks.go:9`, or via the internal uapi field `brokenRoaming`
33	at `device/uapi.go:263`. Neither path is exposed. The flag is designed for
34	"broken mobile semantics" on iOS/Android, not for general use.
35
36	The kernel module behaves identically. Same protocol. Same author.
37
38	## Why this becomes split-brain in our setup
39
40	Architecture facts:
41
42	- `home.goodkind.io` Cloudflare DNS-LB rotates between AT&T-side address
43	(`2600:1700:2f71:c80::1`) and Webpass-side address (`2604:5500:c271:be00::1`).
44	- Suburban dials `home.goodkind.io:51820` on a periodic basis. DNS resolution
45	happens per dial, not cached forever.
46	- VM 113 has DNAT rules for both AT&T and Webpass IPv6 addresses. Both forward
47	to OPNsense `:fe::2:51820`.
48	- VM 113 mangle prerouting: inbound iif sets ct mark, so reply egresses the
49	same WAN suburban dialed. This is DNS-LB symmetry. It works for general
50	traffic.
51	- VM 113 mangle prerouting also has a mod-2 random LB rule for OPNsense-initiated
52	outbound: `ip6 saddr :fe::2 ct state new mark set numgen random mod 2`.
53
54	Two flows that break consistency:
55
56	### Flow A: suburban dials, then OPNsense rekeys
57
58	1. Suburban resolves `home.goodkind.io`. Cloudflare returns AT&T address.
59	2. Suburban dials `[2600:1700:2f71:c80::1]:51820`.
60	3. VM 113 DNATs to OPNsense. OPNsense's wg sees endpoint `:7bf2`, suburban's
61	  public Comcast SLAAC.
62	4. Inbound iif rule on VM 113 marks the conntrack AT&T (`mark=1`).
63	5. OPNsense replies. Reply ct state established. Mark restored from ct. AT&T.
64	6. Suburban's wg sees authenticated reply with src `:c80::1` after SNAT
65	  reverse. `SetEndpointFromPacket` keeps suburban's stored endpoint at
66	   `:c80::1`. Consistent with what it dialed.
67
68	So far healthy.
69
70	1. Two minutes later, OPNsense initiates a rekey. The rekey-after timer
71	  fires. New conntrack from `:fe::2`. Mod-2 rule fires. 50% chance: mark 2
72	   (Webpass).
73	2. Reply egresses Webpass. Postrouting NAT rewrites src to `:be00::1`.
74	3. Packet arrives at suburban. Suburban's wg validates the packet. Key matches.
75	  Then it calls `SetEndpointFromPacket` with the new source `:be00::1`.
76	   Suburban's stored endpoint is now `:be00::1`.
77	4. Suburban's next periodic re-resolution of `home.goodkind.io` is irrelevant.
78	  wg only re-resolves the configured `Endpoint=` line at startup and on
79	    persistent-keepalive failure. Roaming-set endpoints do not get re-resolved.
80	5. From now on, suburban initiates to `:be00::1`. If Webpass IPv6 path has
81	  any flakiness, the WG session degrades silently.
82
83	### Flow B: peer IP renumber
84
85	Suburban's Comcast `:7bf2` /64 changes (SLAAC renumber, modem reboot, lease
86	rotation). Suburban's outbound packets now come from a new source IP.
87	OPNsense receives, authenticates, calls `SetEndpointFromPacket`. Its stored
88	endpoint for suburban now updates to the new IP. OPNsense-side stays
89	consistent.
90
91	But until the next OPNsense-initiated send (rekey or persistent-keepalive),
92	suburban-side has not received anything from OPNsense via the new-source
93	flow. Suburban-side endpoint for OPNsense is unchanged. Symmetric to flow A.
94
95	If during the renumber window Comcast's modem stateful firewall conntrack
96	maps the old src and new src differently, or upstream Webpass and AT&T reply
97	paths have different reachability to the new prefix, then split-brain occurs.
98	Each side validly sees its peer at a different IP. Each side's outbound
99	continues to that stored IP. Asymmetric routing kills the session.
100
101	### Flow C: VM 113 reboots or conntrack flush
102
103	If VM 113 mwan-router reboots, all flows from `:fe::2` become "ct state new"
104	again on next packet. The mod-2 random LB rule re-runs. If suburban's stored
105	endpoint at the time was `:c80::1` but the new mod-2 picks Webpass, OPNsense's
106	reply egresses Webpass. Suburban's roaming sets endpoint to `:be00::1`. Flow
107	continues asymmetric to what suburban DIALS via its `Endpoint=` config. One
108	cycle later if mod-2 picks AT&T again, endpoint moves back. Endpoint flapping
109	in lockstep with mod-2 randomness on every conntrack flush.
110
111	## What CAN'T fix it
112
113	- **Pinning OPNsense-initiated to AT&T at VM 113** (commit `e2e17a1` shipped
114	earlier). Does not help because most traffic is suburban-initiated.
115	OPNsense-initiated rekeys also exist, but they only hit `ct state new` in
116	the brief window after conntrack expiry. The existing flow is usually in
117	`ct state established` from suburban's prior dial. So the pin rule rarely
118	fires. Effectively inert in practice.
119	- **Disabling roaming**. Not exposed in stock wg/wg-quick uapi. Would require
120	custom-built wireguard-go. Not feasible.
121	- **Static `Endpoint=` on suburban config**. Defeats DNS-LB failover. Goes
122	against active-active intent.
123	- **Pinning inbound on VM 113 to a single WAN regardless of iif**. Defeats
124	DNS-LB symmetry. Breaks all DNS-LB-routed traffic, not just WG.
125
126	## Detection plus manual reconciliation
127
128	Run wghealth on BOTH sides. On endpoint mismatch (post-NAT-normalization),
129	alert. Operator restarts wg-quick on one side. Both sides converge.
130
131	Pros: zero protocol-level change, observable.
132	Cons: requires manual intervention. The whole point of WG is "set and forget."
133
134	## Implementation note for bidirectional wghealth
135
136	Today's `wg_health` ([mwan/go/internal/ifmgr/modules/wghealth/](../../mwan/go/internal/ifmgr/modules/wghealth/)) polls OPNsense via SSH.
137
138	Two paths can add the suburban side:
139
140	- **Local-exec mode**. Run `mwan-ifmgr` on suburban with a `suburban-wg` role
141	  and a wghealth instance that reads the local WireGuard interface. Each daemon
142	  emits per-peer logs from its own viewpoint. Cross-checking happens by log
143	  analysis or a correlation layer.
144	- **Remote SSH mode**. Extend wghealth to support multiple SSH targets. The
145	  vault daemon polls both OPNsense and suburban over SSH. `wg` is only readable
146	  via root or the `wg-quick` group.
147
148	Local-exec keeps each box responsible for its own observation. Remote SSH keeps
149	one daemon responsible for both viewpoints but requires credentials and read
150	permissions on each target.
151

1	# Berylax
2
3	## Current status
4
5	Berylax is indefinitely offline for now. Treat the berylax USB-serial OOB path,
6	the `home-berylax` Cloudflare tunnel, and the `3d06:bad:b01:300::/64` WARP route
7	as unavailable until a fresh live check says otherwise.
8
9	Broader infrastructure state lives in [overview.md](overview.md).
10
11	## Historical host notes
12
13	Berylax was an OpenWrt 24.10.5 GL.iNet device on the Monkeybrains L2 segment.
14	Its `eth0` interface used Monkeybrains IPv4 and provider SLAAC on the WAN /64.
15	Its `br-lan` interface used `3d06:bad:b01:300::1/64` as a static fake GUA LAN
16	address.
17
18	Berylax used msmtp with SMTP2GO through `/usr/local/bin/send-email` and
19	`/usr/local/bin/send-email-smtp2go`. It was not Ansible-managed.
20
21	The last recorded LAN state used fake GUA `3d06:bad:b01:300::/64` with NPT to
22	the WAN SLAAC /64 on `eth0` and no Monkeybrains PD. `ndppd` proxied WAN-delegated
23	traffic on `eth0`; downstream `3d06:bad:b01:300::100` IPv6 tests received
24	replies. The NPT prerouting rule exempted local WAN addresses with
25	`fib daddr . iif type local accept`, so the router's own WAN address stayed
26	reachable. Inbound IPv4 and IPv6 ping and SSH were verified on 2026-03-28 from
27	CT 116, and inbound IPv6 ping plus SSH were also verified from a local Mac.
28	Laptops reached `3d06:bad:b01:300::1` via the Cloudflare WARP route on tunnel
29	`home-berylax`, not via OPNsense. No WireGuard was installed.
30
31	## Historical OOB serial path
32
33	The berylax OOB serial path is unavailable while berylax is offline. These notes
34	record the last known procedure.
35
36	When vault's IPv6 network is unreachable, the berylax USB-serial adapter was the
37	only in-band path to vault's console.
38
39	**Prerequisites:**
40
41	- berylax was on the Monkeybrains L2 segment, the same physical switch as
42	  vault's server. It was not on the `3d06:bad:b01::/48` management network, so
43	  it could not SSH to vault directly.
44	- A USB-to-serial cable ran from berylax (`/dev/ttyUSB0`) to vault's physical
45	  serial port.
46	- vault had a serial console configured at 115200 8N1. Proxmox's GRUB and the
47	  Linux kernel both output to this port.
48
49	**Preferred tool: `serial-exec`** ([github.com/agoodkind/serial-exec](https://github.com/agoodkind/serial-exec)).
50	Rust CLI that runs on berylax (static arm64 musl binary, no dependencies). Uses a
51	sentinel-based protocol for reliable output capture and exit code extraction over serial.
52
53	```bash
54	ssh berylax '/tmp/serial-exec run vault "qm list" --json'
55	ssh berylax '/tmp/serial-exec shell vault'
56	ssh berylax '/tmp/serial-exec ping vault'
57	```
58
59	Config on berylax: `~/.config/serial-exec/hosts.toml`
60
61	```toml
62	[hosts.vault]
63	device = "/dev/ttyUSB0"
64	baud = 115200
65	prompt = '(?m)[#$] $'
66	user = "root"
67	```
68
69	If `serial-exec` is unavailable, fall back to `screen /dev/ttyUSB0 115200` on berylax.
70
71	### Procedure: run commands on vault via serial console
72
73	```bash
74	# 1. SSH into berylax
75	ssh berylax
76
77	# 2. Start a detached screen session logging serial output to a file
78	screen -dmS vault-serial /dev/ttyUSB0 115200
79	sleep 1
80	screen -S vault-serial -X logfile /tmp/vault-serial.log
81	screen -S vault-serial -X log on
82
83	# 3. Send a command, for example press Enter to get a prompt, then run a command
84	screen -S vault-serial -X stuff $'\r'
85	sleep 1
86	screen -S vault-serial -X stuff "qm status 113\r"
87	sleep 3
88
89	# 4. Read the output
90	cat /tmp/vault-serial.log
91
92	# 5. To start a stopped VM:
93	screen -S vault-serial -X stuff "qm start 113\r"
94	sleep 15
95	cat /tmp/vault-serial.log
96	```
97
98	**Notes:**
99
100	- `screen` uses `-X stuff` to send keystrokes to the serial TTY. The `\r` at the
101	  end of each command string is the carriage return.
102	- Output contains ANSI escape codes for color and cursor positioning. The log is
103	  still readable but has control sequences mixed in.
104	- vault's zsh prompt looked like `vault ~ >` in the serial output. If the screen
105	  was blank, pressing Enter once woke it.
106	- picocom and minicom were also available on berylax, but both required a real
107	  PTY for interactive use. Screen's `stuff` approach worked non-interactively
108	  from a script or SSH session.
109	- The serial session persisted as long as berylax was up. `screen -ls` checked
110	  whether one was already running.
111	- If vault was mid-boot, kernel messages scrolled through. The operator waited
112	  for the login prompt or zsh prompt before sending commands.
113
114	**What this covered:**
115
116	- MWAN VM 113 stopped and network down: start it with `qm start 113` via serial.
117	- Vault SSH unreachable but host up: run arbitrary commands via serial.
118	- Vault BIOS and GRUB: visible on serial at boot at 115200 baud.
119
120	**What this did not cover:**
121
122	- vault fully powered off or kernel-panicked: requires physical access or IPMI/iDRAC.
123	- berylax unreachable: no remote OOB path available; physical access to the rack
124	  is required.
125	- JetKVM devices (`vault-jetkvm`, `nas-jetkvm`) are also on the Monkeybrains
126	  segment and may provide an alternate KVM-over-IP console path, though their DNS
127	  names (`vault-jetkvm.goodkind.io`, `nas-jetkvm.goodkind.io`) and credentials
128	  were not confirmed at the last recorded check.
129

1	# VM 950 qm config (suburban testbed)
2
3	## Target args
4
5	VM 950 must run with the following Proxmox `args` field. The watchdog on
6	suburban talks to the in-VM `mwan-agent` over native vsock when this device is
7	present.
8
9	```text
10	args: -device vhost-vsock-pci,guest-cid=950
11	```
12
13	## Ownership
14
15	The Proxmox API gates the `args` field with a hard-coded
16	`$authuser eq 'root@pam'` string compare in `qemu-server`. No role and no ACL
17	can bypass it. Even an API token attached to `root@pam` itself fails because
18	the token authuser is `root@pam!<tokenname>`, not bare `root@pam`.
19
20	Tofu cannot set `args` via the bpg/proxmox provider when the suburban provider
21	alias authenticates with an API token. The
22	[opentofu/suburban/vms.tf](../../opentofu/suburban/vms.tf) resource for VM 950
23	omits `kvm_arguments` for that reason. The provider leaves undeclared fields
24	alone, so `tofu plan` does not flag drift on the live `args` string.
25
26	The Ansible playbook [ansible/playbooks/deploy-testbed.yml](../../ansible/playbooks/deploy-testbed.yml)
27	sets the field idempotently from the `Configure suburban testbed extras` play.
28	The task only runs `qm set` when the live `args` does not already match the
29	target string.
30
31	`args` only takes effect at QEMU process start, so an `args` change requires a
32	cold reboot of VM 950. The playbook prints a notice when it changes the value.
33	Run `qm stop 950` then `qm start 950` to pick up the new args.
34
35	## Verification
36
37	Inside the VM the kernel modules `vmw_vsock_virtio_transport` and `vsock` should
38	be loaded and `/dev/vsock` should be present.
39
40	```shell
41	ssh root@3d06:bad:b01:204::950 'lsmod | grep vsock; ls /dev/vsock'
42	```
43
44	On suburban, after restarting the watchdog, the journal should show
45	`ops transport succeeded` on `channel=vsock`.
46
47	```shell
48	ssh suburban 'systemctl restart mwan-watchdog-testbed; \
49	  sleep 10; \
50	  journalctl -u mwan-watchdog-testbed --since "20 seconds ago" --no-pager | \
51	    grep vsock'
52	```

[tool output]
1	# VM 101 qm config (suburban testbed)
2
3	## Target args
4
5	The suburban OPNsense testbed VM `101` runs with the following Proxmox
6	`args` field. The named `io.goodkind.mwan-opnsense.0` virtio console maps
7	to `/dev/ttyV0.1` inside the OPNsense guest, and the `mwan_opnsense` rc.d
8	service writes that path into `/var/lib/mwan/daemon.toml` before the
9	daemon connects to the host-side bridge over this virtio-serial chardev.
10
11	```text
12	args: -device virtio-serial-pci,id=mwanrpc -chardev socket,id=mwanchr,path=/var/run/qemu-server/101.mwanrpc,server=on,wait=off -device virtserialport,chardev=mwanchr,name=io.goodkind.mwan-opnsense.0
13	```
14
15	## Why Tofu does not manage this field
16
17	The Proxmox API gates the `args` field with a hard-coded
18	`$authuser eq 'root@pam'` string compare in `qemu-server`. No role and
19	no ACL can bypass it. Even an API token attached to `root@pam` itself
20	fails because the token's authuser is `root@pam!<tokenname>`, not bare
21	`root@pam`.
22
23	The bpg/proxmox provider therefore omits `kvm_arguments` from
24	[opentofu/suburban/vms.tf](../../opentofu/suburban/vms.tf) and ownership lives in Ansible. The
25	provider leaves undeclared fields alone, so `tofu plan` does not flag drift on
26	the live `args` string.
27
28	## How Ansible owns this field
29
30	The Ansible playbook [ansible/playbooks/deploy-testbed.yml](../../ansible/playbooks/deploy-testbed.yml)
31	carries an idempotent `qm set` task in the `Configure suburban testbed extras`
32	play.
33	The task only runs `qm set` when the live `args` does not already match
34	the target string. Look for the task tagged `args` named
35	`Set mwanrpc chardev on VM 101 args`.
36
37	`args` only takes effect at QEMU process start, so an `args` change
38	requires a cold reboot of VM 101. The playbook prints a notice when it
39	changes the value. Run `qm stop 101` then `qm start 101` to pick up the
40	new args.
41

1	# Wedge-proof the opnsense chardev drainer: Implementation Plan
2
3	> **For agentic workers:** REQUIRED SUB-SKILL: Use superpowers:subagent-driven-development to implement this plan task-by-task. Steps use checkbox (`- [ ]`) syntax for tracking.
4
5	**Goal:** Make the mwan-opnsense break-glass channel impossible to permanently wedge, and bound the transient.
6
7	**Architecture:** Two invariants in the host drainer (`mwan/go/cmd/mwan/opnsense_drain.go`) and its systemd unit. Invariant 1: the chardev fd never closes while the VM is up, so qemu never strands the descriptor (no permanent wedge). Invariant 2: the chardev reader never blocks on the client, so a hung bridge cannot stall the drain (bounded transient).
8
9	**Tech Stack:** Go (FreeBSD plus linux build), systemd fd-store via `github.com/coreos/go-systemd/v22`, ansible-templated unit.
10
11	## Global Constraints
12
13	- The wedge signature: a CPU at RIP `ffffffff809e3xx` (virtqueue_poll) AND a CPU at `ffffffff80c32c5x` (lock_delay), both HLT=0. Idle: both HLT=1 at `ffffffff810b37c6`.
14	- Never drop chardev BYTES to a surviving client. Dropping bytes corrupts the framed yamux stream (the rejected design from commit 2f5f419). Drop the whole CLIENT instead, and flush queued bytes on a client swap.
15	- Do not change the documented transport invariants in `docs/opnsense/daemon.md` ("What not to touch"): no-op `serialStream.Close`, termios, 8 KiB write cap, ack pacing, yamux keepalive off, named virtio port, TIOCFLUSH-on-open, session-rebuild loop.
16	- All Go build, lint, and test run via `make -C mwan/go check` and `make -C mwan/go test`, never raw go.
17	- Deploy units only via the ansible template path, never hand-edited on hosts.
18
19	---
20
21	## File structure
22
23	- `mwan/go/cmd/mwan/opnsense_drain.go`: reader/writer decouple with a bounded queue and client-swap flush (Task 1); fd-store never-close plus fresh-dial startup guard (Task 2).
24	- `mwan/go/cmd/mwan/opnsense_drain_test.go`: unit tests for both.

27	---
28
29	### Task 1: Decouple the chardev reader from the client write
30
31	The reader must never block on the client. A bounded queue sits between a reader goroutine (always drains the chardev) and a writer goroutine (forwards to the current client). On queue overflow the client is dropped, never the bytes of a surviving session. The queue is flushed on a client swap so a new session never receives the prior session's bytes.
32
33	**Files:**
34	- Modify: `mwan/go/cmd/mwan/opnsense_drain.go` (`drainHub`, `drainChardev`, `setClient`)
35	- Test: `mwan/go/cmd/mwan/opnsense_drain_test.go`
36
37	**Interfaces:**
38	- Consumes: `drainHub` (existing), `net.Conn` client and chardev.
39	- Produces: `drainChardev(ctx, log, hub, chardev) error` keeps its signature. Internally a reader goroutine plus a writer goroutine joined by a bounded `chan []byte`. `drainHub.setClient` flushes the pending queue on swap.
40
41	- [ ] **Step 1: Write the failing test for "reader never blocks on a hung client"**
42
43	```go
44	// In opnsense_drain_test.go. A hung client (never reads) must not stop the
45	// chardev drain: a large guest write still completes.
46	func TestDrainReaderNeverBlocksOnHungClient(t *testing.T) {
47		chLocal, chRemote := socketPair(t)            // chLocal = drainer's chardev side
48		defer chLocal.Close(); defer chRemote.Close()
49		hubClient, hungBridge := socketPair(t)        // hubClient attached; hungBridge never reads
50		defer hubClient.Close(); defer hungBridge.Close()
51		ctx, cancel := context.WithCancel(context.Background()); defer cancel()
52		hub := &drainHub{}
53		hub.setClient(hubClient)
54		go func() { _ = drainChardev(ctx, testLog(), hub, chLocal) }()
55		payload := make([]byte, 4<<20)
56		done := make(chan error, 1)
57		go func() { _ = chRemote.SetWriteDeadline(time.Now().Add(5 * time.Second)); _, e := chRemote.Write(payload); done <- e }()
58		select {
59		case e := <-done:
60			if e != nil { t.Fatalf("guest write stalled, reader blocked on hung client: %v", e) }
61		case <-time.After(6 * time.Second):
62			t.Fatal("guest write did not complete: reader blocked on the hung client (wedge risk)")
63		}
64	}
65	```
66
67	- [ ] **Step 2: Run it and verify it fails.** Run `make -C mwan/go test`. The current blocking `c.Write` stalls the read. Expected: FAIL or timeout.
68
69	- [ ] **Step 3: Implement the decouple**
70
71	```go
72	const drainQueueDepth = 256 // ~16 MiB of 64 KiB chunks; a progressing client never fills it
73
74	// setClient swaps the client and flushes any queued bytes from the prior
75	// session, so a reconnecting bridge never receives the dead session's tail.
76	func (h *drainHub) setClient(c net.Conn) {
77		h.mu.Lock()
78		old := h.client
79		h.client = c
80		for h.toClient != nil { // drain prior-session bytes
81			select {
82			case <-h.toClient:
83				continue
84			default:
85			}
86			break
87		}
88		h.mu.Unlock()
89		if old != nil { _ = old.Close() }
90	}
91
92	func drainChardev(ctx context.Context, log *slog.Logger, hub *drainHub, chardev net.Conn) error {
93		dctx, dcancel := context.WithCancel(ctx); defer dcancel()
94		q := make(chan []byte, drainQueueDepth)
95		hub.mu.Lock(); hub.toClient = q; hub.mu.Unlock()
96		defer func() { hub.mu.Lock(); hub.toClient = nil; hub.mu.Unlock() }()
97		readErr := make(chan error, 1)
98		spawn(ctx, log, "drain reader", func() { // never blocks on the client
99			buf := make([]byte, drainBufSize)
100			for {
101				n, err := chardev.Read(buf)
102				if n > 0 {
103					data := make([]byte, n); copy(data, buf[:n])
104					select {
105					case q <- data:
106					default: // queue full: client hung or too slow. Drop the CLIENT, never a survivor's bytes.
107						if c := hub.getClient(); c != nil { hub.clearClient(c); _ = c.Close() }
108					}
109				}
110				if err != nil { select { case readErr <- err: default: }; return }
111			}
112		})
113		spawn(ctx, log, "drain writer", func() {
114			for {
115				select {
116				case <-dctx.Done(): return
117				case data := <-q:
118					if c := hub.getClient(); c != nil {
119						if _, werr := c.Write(data); werr != nil { hub.clearClient(c); _ = c.Close() }
120					} // no client: discard (the chardev still drains, guest write completes)
121				}
122			}
123		})
124		select {
125		case <-ctx.Done(): return nil
126		case err := <-readErr: return err
127		}
128	}
129	```
130
131	Add `toClient chan []byte` to the `drainHub` struct. Keep `getClient` and `clearClient` as is.
132
133	- [ ] **Step 4: Add the no-byte-loss-to-a-survivor test**
134
135	```go
136	// A client that keeps reading loses no bytes: what the guest writes arrives intact.
137	func TestDrainNoByteLossToReadingClient(t *testing.T) {
138		chLocal, chRemote := socketPair(t); defer chLocal.Close(); defer chRemote.Close()
139		hubClient, bridge := socketPair(t); defer hubClient.Close(); defer bridge.Close()
140		ctx, cancel := context.WithCancel(context.Background()); defer cancel()
141		hub := &drainHub{}; hub.setClient(hubClient)
142		go func() { _ = drainChardev(ctx, testLog(), hub, chLocal) }()
143		msg := bytes.Repeat([]byte("MWN1"), 4096) // 16 KiB
144		go func() { _, _ = chRemote.Write(msg) }()
145		got := readN(t, bridge, len(msg))
146		if got != string(msg) { t.Fatalf("byte loss to a reading client") }
147	}
148	```

[tool: Read] {"file_path":"/Users/agoodkind/Sites/configs.wt-ifmgr-scaffold/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-27-wedgeproof-design.md"}
[tool output]
1	# Wedge-proof the mwan-opnsense break-glass channel
2
3	## Goal
4
5	The OOB serial channel must never PERMANENTLY wedge the OPNsense guest. A brief,
6	self-clearing transient strand is tolerable. A wedge that needs a hard reset is not.
7
8	## Background and physics
9
10	qemu strands the in-flight virtio-serial descriptor only on a host-side chardev DISCONNECT.
11	While the chardev fd stays open, a guest write may spin or block transiently, but qemu
12	completes it once the host resumes reading. Therefore a permanent wedge is impossible as long
13	as the chardev fd never closes while the VM is up.
14
15	Proven this session by sub-second register sampling on testbed VM 101 (qemu 11.0.0):
16
17	- fd-store OFF, drainer `kill -9` mid-pull: the host fd closed on process death, qemu saw a
18	  disconnect, and the guest wedged permanently (the positive control).
19	- fd-store ON, the same `kill -9`: the fd stayed open in the systemd store, so qemu saw no
20	  disconnect. The guest showed the wedge signature for ~2.7s, then self-cleared the instant the
21	  respawned drainer logged "adopted reclaimed chardev fd". Health returned at +8s.
22
23	A qemu-layer fix was ruled out. qemu 11.0.0 still strands; the chardev is a server-mode socket
24	(`server=on,wait=off`); `reconnect=` is client-mode only; there is no flush-on-disconnect flag.
25
26	The wedge signature is a CPU at RIP `ffffffff809e3xx` (virtqueue_poll) AND a CPU at
27	`ffffffff80c32c5x` (lock_delay), both with HLT=0. Idle is both CPUs HLT=1 at `ffffffff810b37c6`.
28
29	## Invariant 1: the chardev fd never closes while the VM is up
30
31	This eliminates the permanent wedge. Requirements:
32
33	- Store the chardev fd in the systemd fd-store before serving, on the fresh-dial path only.
34	- Set `FileDescriptorStorePreserve=yes` on the drainer unit so the store survives a crash and a
35	  stop, not only a clean restart.
36	- Never close the chardev fd on any drainer exit path. The fd outlives the process; only the
37	  next instance reclaims it.
38	- A fresh dial is the only host-side disconnect window. On startup, when the VM is up and a
39	  stored fd should exist but does not, treat it as a real event: log loudly and account for it,
40	  rather than silently dialing fresh and opening a disconnect window.
41	- The fd-store-off positive control becomes a permanent regression guard: with the store
42	  disabled, the same disruption must still produce the wedge signature, proving the store is
43	  load-bearing and the detector can see a real wedge.
44
45	## Invariant 2: the chardev reader never stalls
46
47	This bounds and shrinks the transient. Requirements:
48
49	- The chardev read loop must never block on the client write. A hung bridge (socket open, not
50	  reading) must not stall the read.
51	- Decouple read from write. A reader goroutine always drains the chardev into a bounded buffer.
52	  A writer goroutine forwards to the current client.
53	- On bounded-buffer overflow, drop the CLIENT (close it, force a fresh yamux session), never
54	  drop bytes. Dropping bytes corrupts the framed yamux stream; that was the litigated failure of
55	  the original design (reverted in commit 2f5f419). Dropping the client confines corruption to
56	  the now-dead session, which the client-side transfer stall watchdog and the session rebuild
57	  already cover.
58	- Minimize the respawn gap so a `kill -9` unread window shrinks from ~3s toward sub-second:
59	  `RestartSec` near 0 and minimal startup work before reading resumes.
60
61	## Components
62
63	- `mwan/go/cmd/mwan/opnsense_drain.go`: the reader/writer decouple (invariant 2) and the
64	  fd-store store/preserve/never-close paths plus the fresh-dial startup guard (invariant 1).
65	- The `mwan-opnsense-drain.service` unit and its ansible template:
66	  `FileDescriptorStorePreserve=yes`, `RestartSec` near 0.
67	- Tests: drain unit tests for lossless-under-client-churn, drop-the-client-on-overflow (no byte
68	  loss to a surviving session), and the reader-never-blocks property.
69
70	## Acceptance criteria
71
72	Validated by the sub-second wedge-proof harness (`qmp_sampler.py` at ~60ms cadence plus the
73	matrix driver), both directions at >=256MB cut deep mid-transfer, against every chardev-touching
74	disruption, accumulation with no reset between trials, >=30 genuine trials per cell, per-trial
75	artifacts retained:
76
77	1. Zero PERMANENT wedge: no trial shows the signature sustained until a hard reset. Every trial
78	   recovers on its own to a working exec probe, recover-at recorded over a >=180s window.
79	2. Any transient strand is bounded and self-clearing, with its duration measured from the
80	   sub-second samples.
81	3. Per-restart fd hand-off proven: "adopted reclaimed chardev fd" logged, counted from a
82	   pre-trigger timestamp.
83	4. Positive control still wedges: with the fd-store disabled, the same disruption produces the
84	   sustained signature.
85	5. Hung-client path bounded: a deliberately hung bridge does not stall the chardev read without
86	   bound; the reader keeps draining and the client is dropped.
87
88	## Out of scope
89
90	- A qemu-layer or AF_VSOCK transport change.
91	- Changing the documented transport invariants in docs/opnsense/daemon.md ("what not to touch").
92

1	# Configs secret-redaction hardening Implementation Plan
2
3	> **For agentic workers:** REQUIRED SUB-SKILL: Use superpowers:subagent-driven-development (recommended) or superpowers:executing-plans to implement this plan task-by-task. Steps use checkbox (`- [ ]`) syntax for tracking.
4
5	**Goal:** No `configs` subcommand can print a vault secret; all stdout/stderr is filtered through an overlap-safe redactor installed once in `main`, and `secret` writes its value to a hardened temp file instead of printing it.
6
7	**Architecture:** A dependency-free `internal/redact` package implements an Aho-Corasick automaton that finds every secret occurrence in a stream, merges overlapping/touching spans, and rewrites each merged span to `<redacted:KEY>`. `main` reads and validates the vault before dispatch (fail-closed), then routes `os.Stdout`/`os.Stderr` through two mutex-shared redactors so every command and child process is covered. `secret` writes to a `0600` file in a `0700` temp dir.
8
9	**Tech Stack:** Go 1.26 (module `goodkind.io/configs`, working dir `scripts/`), stdlib only, `gopkg.in/yaml.v3` (already a dep), build/lint via `make build` in `scripts/`.
10
11	---
12
13	## File structure
14
15	- Create `scripts/internal/redact/automaton.go` for the Aho-Corasick build, match, and span type.
16	- Create `scripts/internal/redact/automaton_test.go` for automaton and match unit tests.
17	- Create `scripts/internal/redact/writer.go` for the streaming `Writer`, span merge, `Pattern`, `Validate`, and `MinLen`.
18	- Create `scripts/internal/redact/writer_test.go` for writer, merge, and validate unit tests.
19	- Modify `scripts/internal/vault/vault.go` to add `Values`.
20	- Create `scripts/internal/vault/values_test.go` for the `Values` test.
21	- Modify `scripts/cmd/configs/main.go` to install redactors before dispatch and rewrite `runSecret`.
22	- Modify `scripts/internal/ansible/ansible.go` to delete the bespoke `InventoryDump` redaction and stream directly.
23
24	All commands run from `~/Sites/configs`. `make -C scripts build` is the gate (never `go build`); it runs vet, golangci, format, gocyclo, deadcode, staticcheck-extra, govulncheck and signs. For a fast inner loop during TDD, `cd scripts && go test ./internal/redact/...`. Run `make -C scripts build` before each commit.

28	### Task 1: redact package, span type and Aho-Corasick build
29
30	**Files:**
31	- Create: `scripts/internal/redact/automaton.go`
32	- Test: `scripts/internal/redact/automaton_test.go`
33
34	- [ ] **Step 1: Write the failing test**
35
36	```go
37	package redact
38
39	import (
40		"reflect"
41		"sort"
42		"testing"
43	)
44
45	func TestAutomatonFindsAllOccurrences(t *testing.T) {
46		ac := newAutomaton([][]byte{[]byte("ab"), []byte("bc"), []byte("abVALUE")})
47		got := ac.findAll([]byte("xabcabVALUEx"))
48		// spans are [start,end): "ab"@1, "bc"@2, "ab"@4, "abVALUE"@4
49		want := []span{{1, 3}, {2, 4}, {4, 6}, {4, 11}}
50		sort.Slice(got, func(i, j int) bool {
51			if got[i].start != got[j].start {
52				return got[i].start < got[j].start
53			}
54			return got[i].end < got[j].end
55		})
56		if !reflect.DeepEqual(got, want) {
57			t.Fatalf("findAll = %v, want %v", got, want)
58		}
59	}
60
61	func TestAutomatonEmptyPatterns(t *testing.T) {
62		ac := newAutomaton(nil)
63		if got := ac.findAll([]byte("anything")); len(got) != 0 {
64			t.Fatalf("findAll on empty automaton = %v, want none", got)
65		}
66	}
67	```
68
69	- [ ] **Step 2: Run test to verify it fails**
70
71	Run: `cd ~/Sites/configs/scripts && go test ./internal/redact/ -run TestAutomaton -v`
72	Expected: FAIL (undefined: newAutomaton, span).
73
74	- [ ] **Step 3: Write minimal implementation**
75
76	```go
77	// Package redact replaces secret byte sequences in a stream with a labeled
78	// placeholder, so a command's stdout and stderr never expose secret values.
79	package redact
80
81	// span is a half-open byte range [start,end) covered by a secret occurrence.
82	type span struct {
83		start int
84		end   int
85	}
86
87	// automaton is an Aho-Corasick matcher over a fixed set of patterns. It finds
88	// every occurrence of every pattern in a single pass over the input.
89	type automaton struct {
90		next   []map[byte]int // goto per state
91		fail   []int          // failure links
92		out    [][]int        // pattern lengths ending at each state
93		maxLen int
94	}
95
96	// newAutomaton builds the matcher from pattern byte slices. Empty or nil input
97	// yields a matcher that finds nothing.
98	func newAutomaton(patterns [][]byte) *automaton {
99		ac := &automaton{
100			next: []map[byte]int{{}},
101			fail: []int{0},
102			out:  [][]int{nil},
103		}
104		for _, p := range patterns {
105			if len(p) == 0 {
106				continue
107			}
108			ac.add(p)
109			if len(p) > ac.maxLen {
110				ac.maxLen = len(p)
111			}
112		}
113		ac.buildFailureLinks()
114		return ac
115	}
116
117	func (ac *automaton) add(p []byte) {
118		state := 0
119		for _, b := range p {
120			nxt, ok := ac.next[state][b]
121			if !ok {
122				nxt = len(ac.next)
123				ac.next = append(ac.next, map[byte]int{})
124				ac.fail = append(ac.fail, 0)
125				ac.out = append(ac.out, nil)
126				ac.next[state][b] = nxt
127			}
128			state = nxt
129		}
130		ac.out[state] = append(ac.out[state], len(p))
131	}
132
133	func (ac *automaton) buildFailureLinks() {
134		queue := make([]int, 0, len(ac.next))
135		for _, s := range ac.next[0] {
136			ac.fail[s] = 0
137			queue = append(queue, s)
138		}
139		for len(queue) > 0 {
140			state := queue[0]
141			queue = queue[1:]
142			for b, nxt := range ac.next[state] {
143				queue = append(queue, nxt)
144				f := ac.fail[state]
145				for f != 0 {
146					if _, ok := ac.next[f][b]; ok {
147						break
148					}
149					f = ac.fail[f]
150				}
151				if t, ok := ac.next[f][b]; ok && t != nxt {
152					ac.fail[nxt] = t
153				} else {
154					ac.fail[nxt] = 0
155				}
156				ac.out[nxt] = append(ac.out[nxt], ac.out[ac.fail[nxt]]...)
157			}
158		}
159	}
160
161	// step advances the automaton from state on byte b, following failure links.
162	func (ac *automaton) step(state int, b byte) int {
163		for {
164			if nxt, ok := ac.next[state][b]; ok {
165				return nxt
166			}
167			if state == 0 {
168				return 0
169			}
170			state = ac.fail[state]
171		}
172	}
173
174	// findAll returns every occurrence span over data, scanning from state 0.
175	func (ac *automaton) findAll(data []byte) []span {
176		var spans []span
177		state := 0
178		for i := 0; i < len(data); i++ {
179			state = ac.step(state, data[i])
180			for _, length := range ac.out[state] {
181				spans = append(spans, span{start: i - length + 1, end: i + 1})
182			}
183		}
184		return spans
185	}
186	```
187
188	- [ ] **Step 4: Run test to verify it passes**
189
190	Run: `cd ~/Sites/configs/scripts && go test ./internal/redact/ -run TestAutomaton -v`
191	Expected: PASS (both tests).
192
193	- [ ] **Step 5: Commit**
194
195	```bash
196	cd ~/Sites/configs && make -C scripts build && git add scripts/internal/redact/automaton.go scripts/internal/redact/automaton_test.go && git commit -m "Add Aho-Corasick automaton for multi-secret occurrence matching"
197	```

217	func TestValidate(t *testing.T) {
218		ok := []Pattern{{Value: []byte("0123456789abcdef"), Label: "vault_ok"}} // 16
219		if key, valid := Validate(ok); !valid {
220			t.Fatalf("Validate(16-char) = (%q,false), want valid", key)
221		}
222		short := []Pattern{{Value: []byte("short"), Label: "vault_bad"}}
223		key, valid := Validate(short)
224		if valid || key != "vault_bad" {
225			t.Fatalf("Validate(short) = (%q,%v), want (vault_bad,false)", key, valid)
226		}
227		empty := []Pattern{{Value: []byte(""), Label: "vault_empty"}}
228		if key, valid := Validate(empty); !valid {
229			t.Fatalf("Validate(empty value) = (%q,false), want valid (empty ignored)", key)

232
233	func TestMergeSpans(t *testing.T) {
234		in := []labeledSpan{
235			{span{1, 3}, "a"}, {span{2, 4}, "b"}, {span{4, 6}, "c"}, {span{8, 9}, "d"},
236		}
237		// [1,3)&[2,4) overlap -> [1,4); [4,6) touches end 4 -> merge -> [1,6); [8,9) separate
238		got := mergeSpans(in)
239		want := []labeledSpan{{span{1, 6}, "a"}, {span{8, 9}, "d"}}
240		if !reflect.DeepEqual(got, want) {
241			t.Fatalf("mergeSpans = %v, want %v", got, want)
242		}
243	}

247

251	- [ ] **Step 3: Write minimal implementation**
252
253	```go
254	package redact
255
256	import "sort"
257
258	// MinLen is the shortest secret value that can be redacted safely. A non-empty
259	// value shorter than this would risk masking unrelated output, so the caller
260	// fails closed when one is present.
261	const MinLen = 16
262
263	// placeholderPrefix and placeholderSuffix wrap the matched key in the emitted
264	// placeholder, as <redacted:KEY>.
265	const (
266		placeholderPrefix = "<redacted:"
267		placeholderSuffix = ">"
268	)
269
270	// Pattern is one secret value and the vault key whose value it is. Label is not
271	// secret; Value is.
272	type Pattern struct {
273		Value []byte
274		Label string
275	}
276
277	// labeledSpan is a covered byte range carrying the label to emit for it.
278	type labeledSpan struct {
279		span  span
280		label string
281	}
282
283	// Validate returns the label of the first pattern whose value is non-empty and
284	// shorter than MinLen, with ok=false. Empty values are ignored (they match
285	// nothing). ok=true means every value is safe to redact.
286	func Validate(patterns []Pattern) (badKey string, ok bool) {
287		for _, p := range patterns {
288			if len(p.Value) > 0 && len(p.Value) < MinLen {
289				return p.Label, false
290			}
291		}
292		return "", true
293	}
294
295	// mergeSpans coalesces overlapping or touching spans into single covered
296	// regions. Input order is arbitrary; output is sorted by start and
297	// non-overlapping. Each merged region keeps the label of its earliest-starting
298	// member (ties broken by lexicographically smaller label) so the placeholder is
299	// deterministic. This is what makes overlapping secrets leak-proof: two adjacent
300	// secret spans become one redaction.
301	func mergeSpans(in []labeledSpan) []labeledSpan {
302		if len(in) == 0 {
303			return nil
304		}
305		s := append([]labeledSpan(nil), in...)
306		sort.Slice(s, func(i, j int) bool {
307			if s[i].span.start != s[j].span.start {
308				return s[i].span.start < s[j].span.start
309			}
310			if s[i].span.end != s[j].span.end {
311				return s[i].span.end < s[j].span.end
312			}
313			return s[i].label < s[j].label
314		})
315		out := []labeledSpan{s[0]}
316		for _, cur := range s[1:] {
317			last := &out[len(out)-1]
318			if cur.span.start <= last.span.end {
319				if cur.span.end > last.span.end {
320					last.span.end = cur.span.end
321				}
322				continue
323			}
324			out = append(out, cur)
325		}
326		return out
327	}
328	```
329
330	- [ ] **Step 4: Run test to verify it passes**
331
332	Run: `cd ~/Sites/configs/scripts && go test ./internal/redact/ -run 'TestValidate|TestMergeSpans' -v`
333	Expected: PASS.
334
335	- [ ] **Step 5: Commit**
336
337	```bash
338	cd ~/Sites/configs && make -C scripts build && git add scripts/internal/redact/writer.go scripts/internal/redact/writer_test.go && git commit -m "Add redact Pattern, Validate, and overlap-safe span merge"
339	```

353	```go
354	func redactAll(t *testing.T, patterns []Pattern, chunks ...string) string {
355		t.Helper()
356		var sb strings.Builder
357		w := New(&sb, patterns)
358		for _, c := range chunks {
359			if _, err := w.Write([]byte(c)); err != nil {
360				t.Fatalf("Write: %v", err)
361			}
362		}
363		if err := w.Close(); err != nil {
364			t.Fatalf("Close: %v", err)
365		}
366		return sb.String()
367	}
368
369	func TestWriterRedactsSingle(t *testing.T) {
370		pats := []Pattern{{Value: []byte("supersecretvalue1234"), Label: "vault_token"}}
371		got := redactAll(t, pats, "x=supersecretvalue1234;")
372		if got != "x=<redacted:vault_token>;" {
373			t.Fatalf("got %q", got)
374		}
375	}
376
377	func TestWriterRedactsAcrossChunks(t *testing.T) {
378		pats := []Pattern{{Value: []byte("supersecretvalue1234"), Label: "vault_token"}}
379		got := redactAll(t, pats, "x=supersecret", "value1234;")
380		if got != "x=<redacted:vault_token>;" {
381			t.Fatalf("got %q", got)
382		}
383	}
384
385	func TestWriterOverlapNoLeak(t *testing.T) {
386		pats := []Pattern{
387			{Value: []byte("SECRETabcdefghij"), Label: "vault_a"}, // 16
388			{Value: []byte("abcdefghijVALUE1"), Label: "vault_b"}, // 16, shares abcdefghij
389		}
390		got := redactAll(t, pats, "xSECRETabcdefghijVALUE1x")
391		if strings.Contains(got, "VALUE1") || strings.Contains(got, "SECRET") {
392			t.Fatalf("overlap leaked a secret fragment: %q", got)
393		}
394	}
395
396	func TestWriterEmptyPatternsPassthrough(t *testing.T) {
397		got := redactAll(t, nil, "nothing to hide here")
398		if got != "nothing to hide here" {
399			t.Fatalf("got %q", got)
400		}
401	}
402	```

409	- [ ] **Step 3: Write minimal implementation**

411	Append to `scripts/internal/redact/writer.go`. Merge `bytes` and `io` into the existing import block (which already imports `sort`).
412
413	```go
414	// Writer wraps an io.Writer and rewrites every secret occurrence to
415	// <redacted:KEY>. It is streaming-safe: a secret split across Write calls is
416	// still caught, because the trailing maxLen-1 bytes are held back until enough
417	// input arrives or Close is called. A Writer is not safe for concurrent use by
418	// multiple goroutines; the caller serializes writes.
419	type Writer struct {
420		dst      io.Writer
421		ac       *automaton
422		patterns []Pattern
423		buf      []byte
424		maxLen   int
425	}
426
427	// New returns a Writer over dst. An empty pattern set makes the Writer a
428	// transparent passthrough.
429	func New(dst io.Writer, patterns []Pattern) *Writer {
430		values := make([][]byte, 0, len(patterns))
431		maxLen := 0
432		for _, p := range patterns {
433			values = append(values, p.Value)
434			if len(p.Value) > maxLen {
435				maxLen = len(p.Value)
436			}
437		}
438		return &Writer{
439			dst:      dst,
440			ac:       newAutomaton(values),
441			patterns: patterns,
442			maxLen:   maxLen,
443		}
444	}
445
446	// Write buffers p, redacts the portion that can be resolved, and emits it,
447	// holding back the trailing maxLen-1 bytes that a later write might extend.
448	func (w *Writer) Write(p []byte) (int, error) {
449		if w.maxLen == 0 {
450			if _, err := w.dst.Write(p); err != nil {
451				return 0, err
452			}
453			return len(p), nil
454		}
455		w.buf = append(w.buf, p...)
456		hold := w.maxLen - 1
457		if len(w.buf) <= hold {
458			return len(p), nil
459		}
460		safe := len(w.buf) - hold
461		if err := w.flush(w.buf[:safe]); err != nil {
462			return 0, err
463		}
464		w.buf = append(w.buf[:0], w.buf[safe:]...)
465		return len(p), nil
466	}
467
468	// Close redacts and emits any held-back tail.
469	func (w *Writer) Close() error {
470		if len(w.buf) == 0 {
471			return nil
472		}
473		err := w.flush(w.buf)
474		w.buf = nil
475		return err
476	}
477
478	// flush redacts one contiguous slice and writes it to dst. The slice is
479	// self-contained: any match within it is fully inside it, because Write only
480	// passes a region whose held-back suffix cannot start an unresolved match.
481	func (w *Writer) flush(data []byte) error {
482		out := w.redactSlice(data)
483		_, err := w.dst.Write(out)
484		return err
485	}
486
487	// redactSlice finds all occurrences, merges spans, and rewrites covered regions
488	// to their placeholders.
489	func (w *Writer) redactSlice(data []byte) []byte {
490		rawSpans := w.ac.findAll(data)
491		if len(rawSpans) == 0 {
492			return data
493		}
494		labeled := make([]labeledSpan, 0, len(rawSpans))
495		for _, s := range rawSpans {
496			labeled = append(labeled, labeledSpan{span: s, label: w.labelFor(data, s)})
497		}
498		merged := mergeSpans(labeled)
499		var out bytes.Buffer
500		prev := 0
501		for _, ls := range merged {
502			out.Write(data[prev:ls.span.start])
503			out.WriteString(placeholderPrefix + ls.label + placeholderSuffix)
504			prev = ls.span.end
505		}
506		out.Write(data[prev:])
507		return out.Bytes()
508	}
509
510	// labelFor returns the vault key whose value produced span s. The automaton
511	// reports span lengths but not which pattern; match by (length, content)
512	// against the configured patterns, choosing the lexicographically smallest key
513	// on a tie so the placeholder is deterministic.
514	func (w *Writer) labelFor(data []byte, s span) string {
515		value := data[s.start:s.end]
516		best := ""
517		for _, p := range w.patterns {
518			if len(p.Value) == len(value) && bytes.Equal(p.Value, value) {
519				if best == "" || p.Label < best {
520					best = p.Label
521				}
522			}
523		}
524		return best
525	}
526	```
527
528	- [ ] **Step 4: Run test to verify it passes**
529
530	Run: `cd ~/Sites/configs/scripts && go test ./internal/redact/ -run TestWriter -v`
531	Expected: PASS (all four).
532
